        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="efd9f6ca0e344b00a4a4e8b36035ea68">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '491ab57d-36ef-436c-b5ee-1da8e2b3962c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Li/Shengwei">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Qu/Xuanhui" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xuanhui Qu</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">
                     <p class="author-name">Xuanhui Qu</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">

                     </ul>
                     <p>Beijing Advanced Innovation Center for Materials Genome Engineering, Institute for Advanced Materials and Technology, State Key Laboratory for Advanced Metals and Materials, University of Science and Technology Beijing, Beijing, 100083 P.    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d200835497" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Anionic redox chemistry has aroused increasing attention in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) by virtue of the appealing additional capacity. However, up to now, anionic redox reaction has not been reported in the mainstream phosphate cathodes for SIBs. Herein, the ultrathin VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets are fabricated as promising cathodes for SIBs, where the oxygen redox reaction is first activated accompanied by reversible ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> (from the electrolyte) insertion/extraction. As a result, the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode harvests a record-high capacity (168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C) among its counterparts ever reported. Moreover, the ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion efficiently expands the interlayer spacing of VOPO<sub>4</sub> and accelerates the ion diffusion, enabling an unprecedentedly high rate performance (69 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 C). Via systematic ex&nbsp;situ characterizations and theoretical computations, the anionic redox chemistry and charge storage mechanism upon cycling are thoroughly elucidated. This study opens up a new avenue toward high-energy phosphate cathodes for SIBs by triggering anionic redox reactions.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202302200-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202302200-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have captured extensive attention as prospective large-scale energy storage devices and low-speed electric vehicle power sources by virtue of the abundant and low-cost sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0001_R_d200835298e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0004_R_d200835298e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, compared with lithium-ion batteries, SIBs display relatively low energy density resulting from the higher redox potential and larger mass of Na<sup>+</sup> than those of Li<sup>+</sup>, which hinders the widespread industrialization of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0005_R_d200835298e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0006_R_d200835298e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering that the cost and electrochemical performance of batteries are largely governed by cathode materials, it is crucial to excavate high-specific-energy cathodes for accelerating the commercialization of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0007_R_d200835298e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among the potential sodium-storage cathode candidates, such as polyanionic compounds, layered transition metal oxides, organic materials, and Prussian blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0008_R_d200835298e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0012_R_d200835298e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> layered vanadyl phosphate (VOPO<sub>4</sub>) is particularly intriguing due to its robust polyanionic framework enabling the facile 2D Na<sup>+</sup> transport. Moreover, the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox couple provides a high theoretical capacity of 166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a desirable operating potential of ≈3.4&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) with one Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction per formula unit.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0013_R_d200835298e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0014_R_d200835298e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Mater. Today Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the reactivity of VOPO<sub>4</sub> is usually limited by the partial reduction of V<sup>5+</sup> during the synthesis process, reducing the amount of inserted Na<sup>+</sup> upon the first discharge and thus leading to an inferior actual capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0015_R_d200835298e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0016_R_d200835298e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Power Sources">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To address this issue, Cao's group directly synthesized a layered NaVOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode material by chemically pre-intercalating Na ions in VOPO<sub>4</sub>, delivering a promising discharge capacity of 144 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0017_R_d200835298e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Yu's team expanded the interlayer spacing of VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets (from 0.74 to 1.06&nbsp;nm) to ameliorate the sodium-ion diffusion via controlled organic molecules intercalation, achieving an improved reversible capacity of 155 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0018_R_d200835298e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Manthiram and co-workers effectively enhanced the electrical conductivity of VOPO<sub>4</sub> by incorporating reduced graphene oxide, and fascinating rate performance of 72 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C was demonstrated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0019_R_d200835298e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite encouraging progresses have been made, the above-mentioned studies mainly focused on the structure modification and component design of VOPO<sub>4</sub>. The conceptual breakthrough in Na-storage mechanism has not yet been achieved to significantly increase the energy density of VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode. In addition to the traditional transition metal (e.g., vanadium) redox reactions, anionic redox (oxygen redox) chemistry has attracted a great interest in layered oxide cathodes and emerges as a paradigmatic avenue to provide appealing extra-capacity at high voltage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0020_R_d200835298e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Sci. China Chem.">20</a>-<a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0022_R_d200835298e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Joule">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, to the best of our knowledge, anionic redox chemistry has not been deciphered in the mainstream phosphate cathodes for SIBs until now. Interestingly, compared with the strong P<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds in the “PO<sub>4</sub>” configuration of phosphate materials, the lone-pair oxygen electrons in the “VO” configuration of VOPO<sub>4</sub> provides the possibility for oxygen redox reaction. It is thereby highly desirable to activate the anionic redox in VOPO<sub>4</sub> to boost its energy density to a new level.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report, for the first time, the reversible oxygen redox reactions in the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode for SIBs. Uniformly distributed ultrathin VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets are synthesized via a one-step hydrothermal method coupled with the liquid-exfoliation process. Oxygen redox in VOPO<sub>4</sub> is successfully triggered during the initial charge process accompanied by ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> (from the electrolyte) insertion, and this mechanism is reversibly implemented in the following cycles as proved by a combination of ex&nbsp;situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS), Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy, electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) analyses, and first-principles calculations. Accordingly, the as-prepared VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode achieves a record-high discharge capacity (168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C) among its counterparts ever reported, as well as an unprecedented rate capability (69 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 C) and exceptional cycling stability (capacity retentions of 95% after 100 cycles at 0.5 C and 87% after 1000 cycles at 5 C). Moreover, the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode exhibits rapid ion diffusivity and desirable pseudocapacitance contribution, even in the high-voltage region of oxygen redox, which are elaborated by multiple electrochemical measurements including galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), in&nbsp;situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and variable-scan-rate cyclic voltammograms (CV). Finally, the soft-package sodium-ion full cell assembled by the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets cathode and the pre-sodiated hard carbon anode displays a prominent energy density (330.3&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>) and respectable low-temperature tolerance (98.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at −20 °C, 0.5 C), manifesting a bright future for practical applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202302200-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202302200-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets were fabricated through the liquid-phase exfoliation of bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O in isopropanol, where the bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O was prepared by a facile hydrothermal method (detailed in Supporting Information). As schematized in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1a</a>, H<sub>2</sub>O molecules escape from the interlayers of VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O owing to the formation of hydrogen bonds between isopropanol and water, and the original tightly stacked layers are highly exfoliated under the ultrasonication treatment. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1b</a> show that all the characteristic peaks of bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O are well indexed into the standard card of the tetragonal phase (JCPDS No.84-0111, space group of <i>P</i>4/<i>nmm</i>). After the exfoliation process, the (00<i>l</i>) peaks mildly shift rightward, indicating a slightly reduced interlayer spacing due to the loss of H<sub>2</sub>O molecules. Meanwhile, the (00<i>l</i>) peaks significantly weaken and the (<i>h</i>0<i>l</i>) peaks disappear because the thickness in the <i>c</i>-axis greatly decreases, attesting the successful exfoliation of VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0023_R_d200835298e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Lett.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1c</a> presents the Raman spectra of VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and VOPO<sub>4</sub>, where the peaks centered at 550, 690, 940, 990, and 1040 cm<sup>−1</sup> for VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O are assigned to the P<span class="icomoon"></span>O symmetric bending vibration, V<span class="icomoon"></span>O, P<span class="icomoon"></span>O, and V=O stretching vibrations, respectively. The obvious redshift of the P<span class="icomoon"></span>O stretching band for VOPO<sub>4</sub> further confirms the dehydration process since the extraction of interlayer H<sub>2</sub>O molecules effectively alleviates the steric hindrance of P<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0024_R_d200835298e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the decrease and broadening of this peak are related to the lowered polarizability of PO<sub>4</sub> due to the broken hydrogen bonds between the water molecules and the oxygen atoms in PO<sub>4</sub> groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0025_R_d200835298e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thermogravimetric analyses also prove the removal of interlayer water molecules in the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0018_R_d200835298e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Scanning electron microscope (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1d,e</a>) display that the exfoliated VOPO<sub>4</sub> appears as uniformly dispersed ultrathin nanosheets with thicknesses of 20−30&nbsp;nm and sizes of 500−800&nbsp;nm, much smaller than those of the bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O flakes (thicknesses of 100−300&nbsp;nm and sizes of 9−12&nbsp;µm, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1e</a> shows a colorless and transparent colloidal suspension with the Tyndall effect when VOPO<sub>4</sub> was dispersed in isopropanol, intuitively demonstrating the ultrathin thickness and nanoscale size of the exfoliated VOPO<sub>4</sub>. High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1f</a>) depicts the lattice stripe with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.31&nbsp;nm that corresponds to the (200) plane, as is also confirmed by the fast Fourier transform (FFT) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1f</a> inset). The energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1g</a> verify the homogeneous distribution of V, O, and P elements throughout the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets, and the XPS survey spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) manifests the good purity of the product. To preliminarily investigate the anionic redox activity of VOPO<sub>4</sub>, density functional theory (DFT) computations were conducted. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1h</a> shows the density of states (DOS) of VOPO<sub>4</sub>, where the highest occupied bands near the Fermi level (E<sub>f</sub>) are dominated by the O 2p states, suggesting that oxygen ions should be the main electron donors upon the charging process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0026_R_d200835298e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Environ. Sci.">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0027_R_d200835298e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electron localization function (ELF) map in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1i</a> reveals the existence of lone-pair oxygen electrons along the V=O bonds of VOPO<sub>4</sub>, as also confirmed by the distinct lobes on the oxygen pointing toward vanadium cations (marked by red circles) in the ELF isosurfaces (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0001">1j</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0028_R_d200835298e744" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Joule">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The nonbonding O 2p states in the “VO” configuration (rather than the “PO<sub>4</sub>” configuration) testify the high reactivity of oxygen redox in VOPO<sub>4</sub>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202302200-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c1c642ee-c053-42b1-ad05-954807bcd104/adfm202302200-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c1c642ee-c053-42b1-ad05-954807bcd104/adfm202302200-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aeae4ad6-1bf6-442f-ac41-4b78cc0736ad/adfm202302200-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c1c642ee-c053-42b1-ad05-954807bcd104/adfm202302200-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202302200-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202302200" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the exfoliation process from VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O to VOPO<sub>4</sub>. b) XRD patterns and c) Raman spectra of the bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and VOPO<sub>4</sub>. d) SEM, e) TEM, f) HRTEM, and g) SEM-EDS mapping images of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets. Theoretically computational h) DOS, i) ELF map, and j) ELF isosurfaces of VOPO<sub>4</sub>. ELF = 1 (red) and 0 (blue) indicate the electron localization and delocalization, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Na-storage behaviors of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets were evaluated in sodium half-cells using the NaClO<sub>4</sub>/propylene carbonate (PC)-based electrolyte. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0002">2a</a> shows the CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> within 2.0−4.3&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, two pairs of redox peaks located at 3.05/3.28&nbsp;V and 3.37/3.76&nbsp;V are assigned to the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> redox reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0029_R_d200835298e813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, an apparent oxidation peak at 3.95&nbsp;V is detected in the initial anodic scan (this peak merges into the broad V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> peak in subsequent cycles), which is attributed to the oxygen redox reaction (as will be validated later). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0002">2b</a> plots the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of VOPO<sub>4</sub> at 0.1 C (1 C = 166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). A small plateau representing the oxygen oxidation emerges upon the first charge process, in good agreement with the CV results. Given that no Na ions exist in the as-prepared compound, the anions (ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>) from electrolyte should serve as the charge carriers to maintain the electric neutrality. The VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode delivers a first charge capacity of 60.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to 0.37 ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion per formula unit. Since the V<sup>5+</sup> center in VOPO<sub>4</sub> cannot be further oxidized and the electrolyte decomposes above 4.4&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), the charge capacity mainly originates from the oxygen redox contribution. Benefiting from the synergetic anionic (O<sup>2−</sup>/O<sup>n−</sup>, 0 &lt; <i>n</i> &lt; 2) and cationic (V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup>) redox chemistry, the initial discharge capacity can reach 168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which, as far as we know, is the highest value among those reported for the VOPO<sub>4</sub>-based SIB cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0013_R_d200835298e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0014_R_d200835298e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Mater. Today Energy">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0017_R_d200835298e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem">17</a>-<a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0019_R_d200835298e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0023_R_d200835298e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Lett.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0029_R_d200835298e864" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0002">2c</a> presents the rate performance of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets, which overwhelmingly outperforms that of bulk VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O because the nanostructure of VOPO<sub>4</sub> and the release of interlayer water molecules effectively facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Even at a high current density of 30 C, the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode still affords a respectable discharge capacity of 69 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), the superior rate capability of the present VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets compared with the previously reported NaVOPO<sub>4</sub>/VOPO<sub>4</sub> sodium-storage cathodes is highlighted in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0002">2c</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0013_R_d200835298e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0014_R_d200835298e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Mater. Today Energy">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0017_R_d200835298e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem">17</a>-<a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0019_R_d200835298e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0023_R_d200835298e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Lett.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0029_R_d200835298e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another extremely appealing property of this VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode is the conspicuous cycling performance, displaying a much higher capacity retention of 95% after 100 cycles at 0.5 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0002">2d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information) than that of the VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O cathode (76% after 100 cycles at 0.5 C). This is because H<sub>2</sub>O molecules in VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O tend to be extracted during cycles, leading to harmful side reactions with the organic electrolyte and thus the fast capacity degradation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Since no corrosion behavior is observed in the cell after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), the good compatibility of electrolyte and metallic cell parts in the present battery system is confirmed. Moreover, the long-term cycling durability of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets is exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0002">2e</a>, showing a high capacity retention of 87% after 1000 cycles at 5 C, which surpasses those of the state-of-the-art NaVOPO<sub>4</sub>/VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathodes for SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0002">2f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0013_R_d200835298e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0014_R_d200835298e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Mater. Today Energy">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0017_R_d200835298e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0018_R_d200835298e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0023_R_d200835298e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Lett.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0029_R_d200835298e948" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202302200-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ea78ef03-61c6-449e-8a99-7749847745ab/adfm202302200-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ea78ef03-61c6-449e-8a99-7749847745ab/adfm202302200-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ed8c1c3e-10d0-4830-b6bc-519b8aad986d/adfm202302200-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ea78ef03-61c6-449e-8a99-7749847745ab/adfm202302200-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202302200-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202302200" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV profiles at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and b) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves at 0.1 C in the voltage window of 2.0−4.3&nbsp;V of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets. c) Rate capability of VOPO<sub>4</sub> and VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, inset shows the comparison between the present VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and previously reported NaVOPO<sub>4</sub>/VOPO<sub>4</sub> Na-storage cathodes. d) Cycling performance of VOPO<sub>4</sub> and VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O at 0.5 C. e) Long-term cycling durability of VOPO<sub>4</sub> at 5 C. f) Comparison of capacity retention during cycling between the present VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and previously reported NaVOPO<sub>4</sub>/VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode materials for SIBs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To unveil the charge storage mechanism of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets, ex&nbsp;situ XRD patterns were collected at selected voltage states (as marked in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3a</a>) in the initial cycle. The (001) peak shifts from 12.48° to 11.76° upon full charge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3b</a>), suggesting an expanded interlayer spacing from 0.709 to 0.753&nbsp;nm due to the increased electrostatic repulsion between adjacent oxygen-containing layers after the ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion. During the following discharge process, the (001) peak progressively moves to high-angle region, reflecting the gradual decrease of interlayer distance owing to the reversible ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> extraction and Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. Post-mortem XRD and SEM results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) manifest that the crystal structure and morphology of the fully charged cathode after 100 cycles are well maintained, attesting the high structural robustness of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3c</a> displays the TEM-EDS mapping images of the pristine, fully charged, and discharged samples. The clear outline of Cl element within the charged electrode validates the successful ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion. After discharging to 2.0&nbsp;V, the absence of Cl signal and the appearance of Na signal disclose the reversible ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> release and the continuous Na<sup>+</sup> uptake, which are also confirmed by the ex&nbsp;situ EDS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). This reversible anion insertion/extraction process is further demonstrated by the ex&nbsp;situ FTIR spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3d</a>), where the Cl<span class="icomoon"></span>O peak at ≈1100&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (orange region) emerges upon charge and disappears upon discharge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0030_R_d200835298e1053" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the ex&nbsp;situ Cl 2p XPS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3e</a>), an apparent peak at 208.5&nbsp;eV related to ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> is witnessed when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V and significantly weakens (the tiny signal originates from the surface electrolyte adsorption) upon discharging to 2.0&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0031_R_d200835298e1065" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the strong ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> signal could still be detected in the fully charged electrode after 2, 5, and 10 cycles, providing a solid evidence for the highly reversible ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion/extraction behaviors. The anion insertion/extraction chemistry of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode can also be extended to PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> employing the NaPF<sub>6</sub>/PC-based electrolyte (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202302200-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bc5ccbfa-cfe3-42e3-9720-34a7f439d976/adfm202302200-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bc5ccbfa-cfe3-42e3-9720-34a7f439d976/adfm202302200-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6b4029e4-db4b-494f-8b6a-1bfdab9b12e9/adfm202302200-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bc5ccbfa-cfe3-42e3-9720-34a7f439d976/adfm202302200-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202302200-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202302200" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Initial charge/discharge curve of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets at 0.1 C between 2.0 and 4.3&nbsp;V, the marked states are selected for the ex&nbsp;situ analyses. Ex&nbsp;situ b) XRD patterns, c) TEM-EDS mapping images, d) FTIR spectra, core-level e) Cl 2p and f) O 1s XPS spectra, g) EELS spectra, and h) EPR spectra of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode at different voltage states. i) Schematic diagram illustrating the oxygen redox chemistry as well as the ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction mechanism in VOPO<sub>4</sub> during the charge/discharge process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Thereafter, multiple ex&nbsp;situ characterizations were systematically carried out to probe the charge compensation mechanism of VOPO<sub>4.</sub> The O 1s XPS spectrum at the pristine state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3f</a>) presents two peaks centered at 531.3 and 532.0&nbsp;eV, which are attributed to the lattice oxygen (O<sup>2−</sup>). When charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, an additional peak at 532.5&nbsp;eV corresponding to the O<sup>n−</sup> species arises, demonstrating the oxidation of oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0032_R_d200835298e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This peak eliminates upon discharging to 2.0&nbsp;V and reappears upon charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, even after 2, 5, and 10 cycles, manifesting the high reversibility of the oxygen redox in VOPO<sub>4</sub>. This point is also confirmed by the quantitative analysis of the O<sup>n−</sup> proportion during cycling (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), meanwhile, it can be observed that the oxygen redox reaction inevitably weakens to some extent after the first cycle (as also detected in previously reported layered oxide cathodes featuring oxygen redox chemistry),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0021_R_d200835298e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0027_R_d200835298e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0033_R_d200835298e1147" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> leading to the differences in charge/discharge curves and CV profiles between the first cycle and subsequent cycles. Besides, the VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O cathode also shows oxygen redox activity as confirmed by the ex situ O 1s XPS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). As for the ex situ V 2p XPS spectra of VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information), the deconvoluted results of V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks indicate the existence of a majority of V<sup>5+</sup> (89%) and a trace of V<sup>4+</sup> (11%) in the pristine electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0024_R_d200835298e1174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The tiny V<sup>4+</sup> species were totally oxidized to V<sup>5+</sup> when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V. Upon discharging to 2.0&nbsp;V, most V<sup>5+</sup> was reduced to V<sup>4+</sup>, where the percentage of V<sup>4+</sup> is quantified to be 79% and the residual V<sup>5+</sup> is 21%. Based on the above XPS analyses, the contributions from V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> and O<sup>2−</sup>/O<sup>n−</sup> redox reactions to the initial charge capacity are identified to be 18.2 and 42.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, to the initial discharge capacity are 131.1 and 36.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Ex&nbsp;situ EELS test was implemented to further monitor the valence changes of VOPO<sub>4</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3g</a>). The V valence slightly increases upon initial charge (due to oxidation of the V<sup>4+</sup> traces in the pristine electrode) as the intensity ratio of L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> slightly decreases. When discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, the V L<sub>3</sub>- and L<sub>2</sub>-edges shift to lower energies with an increase in the L<sub>3</sub>/L<sub>2</sub> ratio, indicating the reduction of V<sup>5+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0034_R_d200835298e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Energy Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results are highly consistent with the ex&nbsp;situ V 2p XPS observations. For O K-edge, the pre-edge peak intensity significantly increases after full charge, suggesting an increment in the unoccupied O 2p states and thus the oxygen oxidation. When fully discharged, the pre-edge peak decreases due to the oxygen reduction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0035_R_d200835298e1234" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochem. Commun.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The stable peak at ≈541&nbsp;eV arises from the hybridization of O 2p and P 3<i>σ</i> states,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0036_R_d200835298e1241" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Phys. Rev. B">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which can be used as a reference to investigate the variations of other peaks during electrochemical cycling. In addition, the reactivity of oxygen redox is also corroborated by the ex&nbsp;situ EPR spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3h</a>). The pristine cathode material features a signal (<i>g</i> = 1.96) deriving from the paramagnetic V<sup>4+</sup>. Upon charging to 4.3&nbsp;V, the V signal vanishes because the diamagnetic V<sup>5+</sup> is EPR silent, while the O<sup>n−</sup> signal (<i>g</i> = 2.007) obviously emerges, verifying the participation of O redox in the charge compensation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0037_R_d200835298e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Commun.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0038_R_d200835298e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Chem. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, the O<sup>n−</sup> resonance disappears and the V<sup>4+</sup> resonance reappears, manifesting the reversible oxygen and vanadium redox reactions. In light of the above discussions, the hitherto unknown oxygen redox chemistry in VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode for SIBs accompanied by reversible ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion/extraction is unveiled and schematically illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0003">3i</a>.</p>
         
         <p>GITT and gradient-scan-rate CV measurements were executed to dig out the root of the outstanding rate performance of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets. As depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0004">4a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), the ion-diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i>-values) of VOPO<sub>4</sub> are in the magnitudes of 10<sup>−8</sup>−10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (calculation details are provided in Supporting Information), higher than those of VOPO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O (10<sup>−9</sup>−10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). The reaction resistances derived from the GITT data are summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0004">4b</a>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0039_R_d200835298e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which maintain at low values over the entire charge/discharge process. Besides, the relatively higher <i>D</i>-values and lower reaction resistances in the high-voltage region reflect the rapid reaction kinetics of the anionic redox process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0004">4c</a> displays the CV profiles at gradient scan rates from 0.1 to 0.7&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. All the CV curves exhibit the analogous shape with the increase in scan rate, affirming the minor polarization of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0040_R_d200835298e1341" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a>-<a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0042_R_d200835298e1341" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the Faradaic intercalation and pseudocapacitance behavior are revealed to collaboratively control the charge storage process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0043_R_d200835298e1349" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Environ. Sci.">43</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0044_R_d200835298e1349" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - eScience">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0004">4d</a>, the pseudocapacitance accounts for 66% of the total current at 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacitive contribution progressively increases as the scan rate rises (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), thereby ensuring the exceptional rate capability of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets. In&nbsp;situ EIS tests were further performed to evaluate the reaction kinetics during the first charge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0004">4e</a>). As the voltage increases, the fitted charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) gradually reduce (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0004">4f</a>) because the enlarged interlayer spacing effectively accelerates the ion diffusion, validating the fast kinetics of the oxygen redox chemistry along with ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202302200-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/274c3fdc-ebc1-4e67-a2cc-7a8aefd149fb/adfm202302200-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/274c3fdc-ebc1-4e67-a2cc-7a8aefd149fb/adfm202302200-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/351e2fbc-5ee7-4396-9abc-da5cd1df44a6/adfm202302200-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/274c3fdc-ebc1-4e67-a2cc-7a8aefd149fb/adfm202302200-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202302200-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202302200" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Charge/discharge GITT curves at 0.1 C within 2.0−4.3&nbsp;V during the second cycle and the corresponding ion-diffusion coefficients of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode. b) Reaction resistances of VOPO<sub>4</sub> derived from the GITT data. c) CV profiles at various scan rates and d) representative CV curve displaying the capacitive contribution (orange area) to the total current at 0.2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. e) In&nbsp;situ EIS plots recorded from 3.8 to 4.3&nbsp;V during the first charge process and f) the corresponding fitted <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> values.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To assess the application feasibility of the tailored VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets, a prototype sodium-ion full battery was assembled by coupling the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode with the pre-sodiated hard carbon (HC) anode (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5a</a>). Given that the HC anode delivers a reversible capacity of ≈290 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information), the active mass ratio of positive to negative electrode was fixed at 1.7:1 to balance the capacity. Based on the CV profiles of the cathode and anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5b</a>), the full-cell voltage window was optimized to be 1.9−4.2&nbsp;V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5c</a> displays that the VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full cell affords an initial discharge capacity of 158 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (based on the active mass on the cathode side) along with an average output voltage of 3.32&nbsp;V, corresponding to a preeminent energy density of 330.3&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the active mass on both the cathode and anode sides). The rate capability of the VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full cell is depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5d</a>, delivering a decent reversible capacity of 60 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 30 C. More impressively, the pouch cell can light up dozens of light-emitting diodes (LEDs, inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5d</a>). The comparisons of the Ragone plots (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5e</a>) and voltage/capacity plots (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information) between the present VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full cell and previously reported HC-based sodium-ion full cells highlight the superiority of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets for practical applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0033_R_d200835298e1469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0041_R_d200835298e1469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0045_R_d200835298e1469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano Energy">45</a>-<a href="#adfm202302200-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202302200-bib-0051_R_d200835298e1469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Storage Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the full cell exhibits a remarkable cycling durability with promising capacity retentions of 94% after 100 cycles at 0.5 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5f</a>) and 82% after 1000 cycles at 5 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5g</a>). To further broaden the utilization scenes, the wide-temperature-range performance of the VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full cell was examined (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202302200-fig-0005">5h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). Even at a low temperature of −20 °C, a fascinating discharge capacity of 98.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C (71.9% of the capacity at 30 °C) was attained, demonstrating the high tolerance in harsh environments of the present VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets cathode.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202302200-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b4e4fd42-648b-4236-bb5a-858a0fbab464/adfm202302200-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b4e4fd42-648b-4236-bb5a-858a0fbab464/adfm202302200-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/adcd8ba8-751c-4b38-9dd2-abd4505ee640/adfm202302200-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b4e4fd42-648b-4236-bb5a-858a0fbab464/adfm202302200-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202302200-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202302200" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic configuration of the VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full battery. b) CV profiles of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and HC anode. c) Typical charge/discharge curves of the VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full cell. d) Rate capability of the VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full battery. Inset shows the LED bulbs lightened by the pouch cell. e) Ragone plots comparing the present VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full cell with previously reported HC-based sodium-ion full cells (based on the total mass of cathode and anode active materials). f) Cycling stability at 0.5 C and g) long-term cycling performance at 5 C of the VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full cell. h) Wide-temperature-range electrochemical test of the VOPO<sub>4</sub>//HC full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202302200-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202302200-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the oxygen redox chemistry in the VOPO<sub>4</sub> Na-storage cathode accompanied by reversible ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> insertion/extraction was firstly deciphered, enabling an admirable energy density beyond the ceiling of the current NaVOPO<sub>4</sub>/VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathodes. Meanwhile, the uptake of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> effectively enlarges the interlayer distance of VOPO<sub>4</sub> and thus accelerates the ion-diffusion dynamics. Consequently, the tailored VOPO<sub>4</sub> nanosheets achieve a superior reversible capacity (168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C), fabulous rate performance (69 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 C), and outstanding long-term cycling stability (87% capacity retention over 1000 cycles at 5 C) in SIBs. Ex&nbsp;situ XRD, EDS mapping, FTIR, XPS, EELS, and EPR analyses were manipulated to elaborate the anionic and cationic redox reactions along with the ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> storage processes. GITT, variable-rate-scan CV, and in situ EIS measurements provide solid evidence for the fast reaction kinetics of the VOPO<sub>4</sub> electrode. More intriguingly, the prototype sodium-ion full battery constructed by the VOPO<sub>4</sub> cathode and pre-sodiated hard carbon anode displays a remarkable energy density of 330.3&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and superb low-temperature tolerance (98.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at −20 °C). This study offers new opportunities for designing high-specific-energy phosphate cathodes of SIBs by initiating anionic redox reactions.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
